An influence on the action, and the Creator of that totality is Allah Almighty, and because this totality necessitates the occurrence of the action, these attributions and ascriptions are correct. And because that totality is a cause for the occurrence of these actions, it is correct that everything is by Allah's decree and by His measure, and by this way the contradiction between the rational proofs and these Quranic proofs is removed. Our master the caller to Allah said: And know: This is only established according to my particular school of thought. Where I said: The combination of power with the motive necessitates the action. Commentary
This passage presents the author's own method for reconciling the attribution of human actions to both God and the servant, thereby resolving the apparent contradiction between rational arguments and Quranic texts. The author begins by stating that God is the creator of the entire set of conditions (the 'totality') that includes the power and motive, and this totality necessitates the occurrence of the act. Because the act follows necessarily from this God-created totality, it is valid to ascribe the act to God (as its ultimate origin) and to the servant (as the one through whose power and choice it occurs). The author then quotes his own school of thought: the combination of power and motive is what necessitates the action. He notes that this solution is only valid for those who accept this principle; those who reject it cannot genuinely affirm the Quranic verses that attribute actions to the servant.

Key Takeaway
The author resolves the decree-action paradox by asserting that God creates the inseparable totality of power and motive, which inevitably yields human action, allowing both divine and human attribution.

The Mu'tazila's Second Method: The Word 'العمل'
Mu'tazila enumerate eight words and give twenty verses for 'العمل'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use twenty Quranic verses containing the word 'العمل' to argue that actions are attributed to the servant, forming part of their broader linguistic proof.

Core Idea
Twenty Quranic verses with 'عمل' show human agency.

The Mu'tazila built their case for human agency on a linguistic argument: every Arabic word for action appears in the Quran attributed to the servant. They listed eight such words, beginning with 'العمل' (work). In this passage, the author presents twenty verses where 'العمل' is clearly ascribed to human beings, showing the Quran's consistent pattern. This evidence is central to their refutation of the view that only God creates actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's claim
Start by reading the opening sentence where they assert that every Arabic action-word is used in the Quran to attribute the act to the servant. Note the eight-word framework.
The eight words are listed but only the first is detailed here.
2
Skim the twenty verses
Read through the list of twenty Quranic verses. Observe the pattern: each verse uses 'عمل' in a context where the action is attributed to a human subject.
Don't memorize the verses; focus on the consistency of attribution.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself: Why would the Mu'tazila think this proves human agency? How might an opponent respond? The commentary will clarify the logic.
This connects to the broader qadar debate.

Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of the Mu'tazila for proving that actions are attributed to the servant. They argue that every word used in Arabic to denote action is also used in the Quran to attribute that action directly to the servant. The author then enumerates eight such words. He begins with the first word, 'العمل' (work), and supports it with twenty Quranic verses, each showing that action is ascribed to the servant. The list includes verses like 'Whoever does a righteous deed, it is for himself' and many others. After listing them, he notes that these are twenty verses just for this one word, and a thorough search would reveal many more in the Quran. The purpose is to counter any claim that actions are only attributed to God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila deny divine decree entirely because they affirm human agency.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not necessarily deny God's ultimate power; they use Quranic evidence to argue that humans have real choice and responsibility, while still acknowledging God's role as creator.

The Second Word: 'الفعل' in the Quran
The author lists Quranic verses that use 'فعل' for human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran uses the word 'الفعل' to attribute actions to the servant, and you will see four examples from the Quran that support this.

Core Idea
The Quran uses 'الفعل' for human actions, not only for God's.

In this passage, the author continues his refutation of the Mu'tazila by examining the second word they use: 'الفعل' (action). He presents Quranic verses that directly attribute actions to the human doer. As you read, notice how each verse clearly addresses the servant as the one who performs the action. This is the first step in showing that the Mu'tazila's linguistic evidence actually supports the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins with 'اللفظ الثاني [ الفعل ]' to introduce the second word.
The passage is cut off at the fourth verse.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
List each verse mentioned: a) 'وما تفعلوا من خير...', b) 'ووفيت كل نفس ما عملت...', c) 'وافعلوا الخير', d) 'والذين هم للزكاة...'. Recognize that the fourth is incomplete.
3

For each verse, note who is being addressed as the doer. In (a) 'تفعلوا' (you do), (b) 'ما عملت' (what it did), (c) 'افعلوا' (do), (d) presumably 'الذين هم للزكاة' (those who are for zakah). All attribute the act to the servant.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this matters: the Mu'tazila claim that 'الفعل' in the Quran shows humans create their own actions. But the Ash'aris argue that these verses only show acquisition (kasb), not creation. This passage starts the evidence for the second word.

The author enumerates Quranic verses affirming human action and concludes with a count.

The Quran repeatedly attributes actions to humans, proving real agency.

To establish that human beings are true agents of their own actions, the author turns to the Quran itself. He lists a series of verses that use clear language of doing, working, and being rewarded for deeds. These verses are not symbolic; they show that God holds people accountable for what they actually do. This passage presents the scriptural foundation for a key point in the debate about qadar.

In this lesson, we examine the third key linguistic term used in the Qur'an to describe human action: aṣ-ṣun‘ (making). By looking at two verses that use this term, the author further supports the idea that humans are genuine agents of their deeds, a central point in the debate on qadar.

The Fourth Term: Al-Kasb (Earning)
Author cites five Quranic verses on al-kasb to affirm human earning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic term al-kasb and how the cited verses affirm that humans earn their actions and are recompensed accordingly.

Core Idea
Al-kasb: actions are earned by humans and recompensed by Allah.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth linguistic term in the discussion of human actions: al-kasb (earning). The author cites five Quranic verses that use the root k-s-b to demonstrate that humans genuinely earn their deeds. These verses tie earning directly to reward and punishment, reinforcing individual responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the term
Read the Arabic passage and note that the author introduces 'al-kasb' as the fourth term. Then look at each verse cited.
2
Examine each verse and notice the common root
For each verse, find the word derived from k-s-b. Observe the pattern: 'kasabat', 'kasab', 'yaksibu'.
All verses emphasize that earning leads to recompense.
3
Reflect on human agency implied by earning
Consider what it means for a human to 'earn' an action. How does this relate to moral accountability?
The verses show that earning results in reward or punishment.
4
Synthesize: theological significance
Summarize: al-kasb affirms that humans are genuine agents of their actions, responsible for what they earn, while Allah is the ultimate creator of actions.
This balances human responsibility and divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ الرابع : الكسب قال تعالى : أ- ووفيت كل نفس ما کسبت (ه) ب ظهر الفساد في البر والبحر بما كسبت أيدي الناس (1) ج کل امریء بما كسب رهين (۲) ) د - كل نفس بما كسبت رهينة(۸) هــ اليوم تجزى كل نفس بما كسبت (۹) )
The fourth term: al-kasb (earning). He said, exalted is He: A - 'And every soul will be fully compensated for what it earned' (5). B - 'Corruption has appeared on land and sea because of what the hands of people have earned' (1). C - 'Every person, for what he earned, is held in pledge' (2). D - 'Every soul, for what it earned, is held in pledge' (8). E - 'Today every soul will be recompensed for what it earned' (9).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage introduces الكسب (earning/acquisition) as the fourth term. Five verses are cited, all using the root كسب to indicate that humans earn or acquire deeds. The verses emphasize individual responsibility: each soul is recompensed for what it has earned, and corruption results from human earning. This reinforces the idea that actions are genuinely attributable to humans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The verses tie kasb directly to reward and punishment, showing that humans are genuine agents accountable for their earned actions.

?

Are you willing to confess this to Allah and change your earning?
?

The Fifth Linguistic Term: al-Ja‘l (Making/Appointing)
The author enumerates three verses to illustrate human use of al-ja‘l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'al-ja‘l' in Arabic is used for both divine and human acts, as demonstrated by three Quranic verses where humans are the subjects of 'making'.

Core Idea
Al-ja‘l is not exclusive to Allah — humans also 'make' and 'appoint'.

We have seen several linguistic terms that support human agency. Now we look at the fifth term, al-ja‘l (making/appointing). The author cites three Quranic verses where the verb 'ja‘ala' is attributed to human beings. This shows that the concept of 'making' or 'appointing' is not restricted to divine action but is part of human capacity.

Guided Path

Verse A: physical action of placing fingers; Verse B: declaring angels female; Verse C: attributing partners to Allah.
3
Reflect on the implication for human agency
Consider why the author includes these verses. They show that humans are capable of 'making' in various ways, supporting the doctrine of human free will and responsibility.
This term is used elsewhere for divine acts, but here it's applied to human acts.

The Sixth Term: al-Khalq and Its Quranic Inference
Quranic evidence for the term al-khalq and its inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's use of the superlative 'أحسن الخالقين' implies that creation (khalq) can be attributed to beings other than Allah in a limited sense.

Core Idea
Superlatives in the Quran imply the existence of others in that category.

This lesson introduces the sixth linguistic term in our series: al-khalq (creation). The author presents a Quranic verse that uses a superlative form arguing that it logically requires the existence of other creators. By examining this inference, we learn how careful linguistic reasoning helps us understand theological concepts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two Quranic references and the analogy provided.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the structure of the inference.
2
Understand the superlative argument
The verse 'أحسن الخالقين' (the best of creators) uses a superlative. In Arabic, a superlative like 'أحسن' (best) implies that there are others who also create, otherwise the comparison would be meaningless. This is a key logical inference.
Think of the analogy: 'Zayd is the best of the virtuous' implies other virtuous people exist.
3
Examine the second verse
The verse addressing Jesus ('إذ تخلق من الطين') uses the verb 'تخلق' (you create) showing that the action of creation can be attributed to a human prophet in a specific context.
This reinforces that the term 'khalq' is used for fashioning or forming, not ex nihilo creation.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some might think this verse attributes divine creation to humans. However, the author clarifies that 'khalq' here means shaping or forming, not creating from nothing. This is a crucial distinction.
The Quran uses 'khalq' in multiple senses; context determines the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ السادس : الخلق . قال الله تعالى : أ - ( فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين (۱) ) وهذا يقتضي كون غيره خالقاً . كقوله : زيد أفضل الفضلاء فإنه يقتضي كون غيره فاضلاب - قال مخاطباً لعيسى : ( وإذ تخلق من الطين
The sixth term: al-khalq (creation). Allah Almighty said: A - 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (1). And this necessitates that others besides Him are creators. Like his saying: 'Zayd is the best of the virtuous' necessitates that others are virtuous. B - He said addressing Jesus: 'And when you create from clay'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage introduces الخلق (creation) as the sixth term. The author cites the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' (best of creators) and makes a logical inference: the superlative form 'أحسن' implies that there are other creators, otherwise the comparison would be meaningless. He gives an analogy: saying 'Zayd is the best of the virtuous' (زيد أفضل الفضلاء) implies there are other virtuous people. Thus, the Quran itself indicates that humans can be described as 'creators' in a certain sense, as in the verse addressing Jesus who creates a bird from clay (إذ تخلق من الطين).

Key Terms
الخلق
أحسن الخالقين
تخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'أحسن الخالقين' means that only Allah is Creator.
Correction: The superlative form actually implies there are other creators; otherwise the comparison would be meaningless. However, the type of creation is different for humans (fashioning) versus Allah (ex nihilo).

Checkpoint
What inference does the author draw from the verse 'أحسن الخالقين'?
A) Allah alone is Creator
B) Others besides Allah are also called creators
C) The verse is metaphorical
D) Humans cannot create anything

Key Takeaway
The Quranic use of a superlative like 'أحسن الخالقين' logically implies that the term 'khalq' can be applied to others besides Allah, though in a different sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you create something—whether it's a piece of art, a meal, or even a new habit—do you see that act as a reflection of Allah's attribute of creation, or do you feel a sense of pride that distances you from Him? How can you transform that pride into gratitude for His original creation?
?
The Quran's argument here relies on a subtle linguistic point. Have you ever rushed through verses like 'أحسن الخالقين' without pausing to appreciate the depth of their logical implication? What is one step you can take this week to study the Quran's language more carefully?
?
If the term 'khalq' applies to you in some sense, then every act of shaping your life—your relationships, your work, your character—is a kind of creation. But Allah is the best of creators. In what area of your life have you settled for mediocrity when you could be seeking His standard of excellence?

The Second Proof from Divine Commands and Prohibitions
The author presents the second argument from Quranic commands and rebuts the objection of incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument from Qur'anic commands that presuppose human ability, and the Ash'arite response that the objection of incapacity actually refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Commands make sense only if the commanded can act—otherwise they are absurd.

The Mu'tazilites argued that the numerous commands in the Qur'an prove humans have free will to obey or disobey. The author presents their argument and then answers it by turning the tables: the very absurdity of commanding someone without ability shows that the Mu'tazilite position leads to the same absurdity, as previously demonstrated.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses of command
Read the nine Qur'anic verses cited in the passage. Notice how each one is a direct command or prohibition to believers.
These verses are used as evidence that commands presuppose the ability to comply.
2
Grasp the logical point
Understand the core argument: if the servant had no power over action or refraining, then commanding and forbidding would be meaningless. The author asks rhetorically, 'How is it intellectually permissible to say: Do, O you who does not do?'
3
Consider the analogy to inanimate objects
The author draws an analogy to commanding inanimate objects—sending prophets to them, revealing books—which everyone recognizes as absurd. This illustrates the foolishness of commanding someone without ability.
This shows that both scripture and reason condemn such commands.
4
Note the author's concise answer
The author does not repeat the earlier refutation but states that the problem of imposing an unbearable obligation (taklif ma la yutaq) is a logical consequence of the Mu'tazilites' own position, and that the twelve arguments against it have already been given.
This answer shifts the burden back to the Mu'tazilites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني من وجوه استدلالهم من كتاب الله تعالى : الأمر والنبي : (أ) وسارعوا إلى مغفرة من ربكم (ب) سابقوا إلى مغفرة من ربكم (ج) أجيبوا داعي الله وآمنوا به (د) استجيبوا الله يا أيها الذين آمنوا اركعوا واسجدوا واعبدوا ربكم (هـ) آمنوا خيراً لكم (و) واتبعوا أحسن ما أنزل إليكم من ربكم (ز) وأنيبوا إلى ربكم (ح) وما آتاكم الرسول فخذوه وما نهاكم عنه فانتهوا (ط) أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول. وبالجملة : فالأوامر والنواهي الواردة في كتاب الله ، لا حد لها ولا حصر ، ولو لم يكن العبد قادراً على الفعل والترك ، لم يقبل أمره ولا نهيه ، وكيف يجوز في العقل أن يقال : افعل يا من لا يفعل ؟ ولو جاز ذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يبعث الأنبياء إلى الجمادات ، وينزل عليهم الكتب ويملأ تلك الكتب من الوعد والوعيد للجمادات؟ وكما أن صريح لفظ القرآن يشهد بأن كل ذلك سفه وعبث ، فكذلك صريح العقل يشهد بأن أمر العبد ونهيه - مع لا قدرة له على الفعل والترك - عبث وسفه . والجواب : إنا بينا في باب الجواب عن شبههم العقلية : إن تكليف ما لا يطاق ، لازم على المعتزلة من أثني عشر وجهاً ، فلا فائدة في إعادتها في هذا المقام وبالله التوفيق
The second aspect of their arguments from the Book of Allah Most High: commands and prohibitions: (a) 'And hasten to forgiveness from your Lord' (b) 'Race to forgiveness from your Lord' (c) 'Respond to the caller of Allah and believe in Him' (d) 'Respond to Allah, O you who have believed, bow and prostrate and worship your Lord' (e) 'Believe, it is better for you' (f) 'And follow the best of what was revealed to you from your Lord' (g) 'And turn in repentance to your Lord' (h) 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it, and whatever he forbids you, refrain from it' (i) 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger'. In summary: the commands and prohibitions in the Book of Allah have no limit or count, and if the servant were not capable of action and refraining, his command and prohibition would not be accepted. How is it intellectually permissible to say: 'Do, O you who does not do'? And if that were permissible, then why is it not permissible to send prophets to inanimate objects and reveal books to them and fill those books with promise and threat for inanimate objects? Just as the explicit wording of the Qur'an testifies that all that is foolishness and frivolity, so too the explicit intellect testifies that commanding and prohibiting the servant—with no power over action and refraining—is foolishness and frivolity. The answer: We have explained in the chapter on answering their rational doubts that imposing an unbearable obligation (taklif ma la yutaq) is incumbent upon the Mu'tazila from twelve aspects, so there is no benefit in repeating them in this context. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument from the Book of Allah used by the Mu'tazila (or perhaps the author is presenting their argument to refute it). The argument is based on the numerous commands and prohibitions in the Qur'an. The author lists several verses that contain imperatives: hasten to forgiveness, respond to Allah, obey, etc. The general point is that commands and prohibitions are meaningful only if the addressee has the ability to obey or disobey. If a person had no power over his actions, then commanding him would be absurd—like commanding an inanimate object. The author argues that both the explicit wording of the Qur'an (which would not speak of such actions in vain) and the intellect confirm that commanding someone without ability is foolishness. The objection (raised by the opponents) is that this argument could be turned around: if ability is required, then how can Allah command what He knows will not happen? The author answers briefly by referring to a previous discussion where it was shown that the doctrine of imposing an unbearable obligation (taklif ma la yutaq) is a logical consequence of the Mu'tazila's own position, not an objection against the Ash'aris. The author declines to repeat the twelve arguments already given.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commands are meaningful even if the addressee cannot act because the command itself is a test.
Correction: The passage shows that commanding someone who cannot act is as absurd as commanding inanimate objects, so such commands cannot be meaningful tests.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite appeal to Qur'anic commands to prove human free will is refuted by showing that the absence of ability makes commands absurd, and the objection itself backfires on the Mu'tazilites.

?

The Third Proof from 'How Can You Disbelieve in Allah?' and the Excuses of the Disbeliever
Mu'tazili argument from verse and reductio excuses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the verse 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' to argue for human agency, and the initial excuses they attribute to the disbeliever under a deterministic framework.

Core Idea
The verse's rebuke only makes sense if disbelief is the slave's own act.

This passage presents the third Qur'anic argument used by the Mu'tazila against divine compulsion. It focuses on the verse 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' and unpacks why this rebuke would be meaningless if Allah Himself created disbelief. Then, in a clever reductio, it lists the excuses a disbeliever could offer if determinism were true. Though the passage is cut off, the first five excuses illustrate the logical consequences of denying human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic and English translation together. Notice that the passage has two clear sections: four arguments from the verse, then the beginning of a list of excuses.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the reasoning that is present.
2
Analyze the four aspects
Identify each of the four aspects that explain why the verse implies disbelief is from the slave. Notice the logical pattern: rebuke for something created by Allah would be absurd.
Each aspect builds on the idea that genuine rebuke requires the person to have the ability to do otherwise.
3
Understand the transition to excuses
The passage shifts from the Mu'tazili argument to a hypothetical: if Allah were the creator of disbelief, the disbeliever could offer excuses. This is a reductio ad absurdum of determinism.
The phrase 'according to the compellers (al-mujbira)' refers to the deterministic view.
4
Examine the first five excuses
List the five excuses given: created disbelief, willed disbelief, known disbelief, compelled disbelief, and created a power that... (cut off). Notice how each appeals to a divine attribute to shift blame.
These excuses are considered absurd by the Mu'tazila, showing determinism leads to unacceptable conclusions.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the verse and the excuses together form a powerful case for human responsibility. The rebuke is only just if the disbeliever truly has a choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث من استدلالات القوم : الآيات الدالة على أنه لا مانع لهم من الإيمان والطاعة ، ولا مجبر لهم على الكفر والمعصية . ولتذكر من هذا الجنس آيات : أحدها : قوله تعالى : ( كيف تكفرون بالله ) ؟ قالوا : هذه الآية دالة على أن الكفر من قبل العبد . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : لو كان تعالى قد خلق الكفر فيهم ، لما جاز أن يقول : كيف تكفرون بالله على سبيل التوبيخ ، كما لا يجوز أن يقول : كيف تسودون وتبيضون وتصحون وتسقمون ؟ الثاني : إن كان خلقهم أولاً للشقاء وللنار ، أو ما أراد بخلقهم إلا الكفر والوقوع في النار فكيف يصح أن يقول موبخاً لهم : ( كيف تكفرون ) ؟ الثالث : كيف يليق بالحكيم أن يقول : كيف تكفرون حال ما يخلق الكفر فيهم ؟ الرابع : إنه تعالى إذا قال للكفار : كيف تكفرون فهل ذكر هذا الكلام توجيهاً للحجة على العبد ، وطلباً للجواب منه ، أو ذكره على سبيل الهزل والعبث ؟ والثاني لا يقول به مسلم. فبقي الأول : فنقول : لو كان موجد الكفر هو الله تعالى ، لكان للكافر أن يقول : عندي اثني عشر وجهاً من وجوه العذر . كل واحد منها مستقل بأن يكون جواباً عن هذا السؤال : فالأول : أنه يقول : يا إله الخلق خلقت الكفر في ، فكيف لا أكون كافراً. مع أني لا أقدر على دفع فعلك ؟ الثاني : أردت الكفر ، وأنا لا أقدر على دفع إرادتك . والثالث : علمت الكفر مني - وعند المجبرة : أن خلاف المعلوم ممتنع الوقوع والرابع : أجبرت على وقوع الكفر - وعند المجبرة ، خلاف خبر الله ممتنع الوقوع والخامس : إنك خلقت في قدرة لا
The third aspect of the people's arguments: the verses indicating that there is no preventer for them from faith and obedience, and no compeller for them to disbelief and disobedience. Let there be mentioned from this type verses: one of them is His saying Most High: 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' They said: This verse indicates that disbelief is from the slave. Its explanation is from aspects: First: If the Most High had created disbelief in them, it would not be permissible for Him to say 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' by way of rebuke, just as it is not permissible to say 'How do you become black and white and healthy and sick?' Second: If He created them first for misery and for the Fire, or He did not intend by creating them except disbelief and falling into the Fire, then how is it correct for Him to say rebuking them: 'How can you disbelieve?' Third: How is it befitting for the Wise to say 'How can you disbelieve?' while He creates disbelief in them? Fourth: When the Most High says to the disbelievers 'How can you disbelieve?', did He mention this speech as a presentation of the proof against the slave and a request for an answer from him, or did He mention it by way of jest and frivolity? The second no Muslim says. So the first remains. We say: If the originator of disbelief is Allah Most High, then the disbeliever would be able to say: I have twelve aspects of excuse, each one of them is independent to be an answer to this question: The first: He says: O Creator of creation, You created disbelief in me, so how can I not be a disbeliever, while I cannot repel Your action? The second: You willed disbelief, and I cannot repel Your will. The third: You knew disbelief from me—and according to the compellers (al-mujbira): opposing the known is impossible of occurrence. The fourth: You compelled the occurrence of disbelief—and according to the compellers: opposing the news of Allah is impossible of occurrence. The fifth: You created in me a power that...
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument from the Qur'an, specifically from the verse 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' (Qur'an 2:28). The argument is attributed to 'the people' (al-qawm), likely the Mu'tazila, who use this verse to prove that disbelief originates from the human slave, not from Allah. The explanation proceeds through four points. 1) If Allah had created disbelief in them, then rebuking them for it would be as absurd as rebuking someone for something involuntary like skin color or health. 2) If Allah created them for misery and intended disbelief, the rebuke would be inappropriate. 3) It is not befitting for the Wise to rebuke for something He Himself creates. 4) The rebuke is presented as a serious demand for an answer, not as jest; since it is serious, it implies the disbeliever could have done otherwise. The author then presents a hypothetical response from the disbeliever (a reductio ad absurdum) listing twelve excuses (only the first five are given here due to the cut-off). The excuses include: Allah created disbelief in him, Allah willed disbelief, Allah knew disbelief, Allah compelled disbelief, and Allah created a limited power. The phrase 'according to the compellers (al-mujbira)' refers to the determinists (the Ash'aris from the Mu'tazila perspective). The passage ends mid-sentence with 'power that [does not]...'

Key Terms
آية
توبيخ
كفر
عذر
مجبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' proves that Allah compels disbelief, because He questions the disbelievers.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue exactly the opposite: the verse rebukes disbelievers, which only makes sense if they have the ability to choose faith. If Allah compelled disbelief, the rebuke would be unjust and absurd.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first aspect explaining why the verse 'How can you disbelieve?' indicates disbelief is from the slave?
A) Allah created disbelief in them, so rebuke is pointless.
B) If Allah created disbelief, the rebuke would be as absurd as rebuking for skin color.
C) The verse is a joke.
D) The disbeliever can offer twelve excuses.

Key Takeaway
The verse's rebuke only makes sense if humans have free will; otherwise, the disbeliever could simply blame Allah, as shown by the listed excuses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever catch yourself making internal excuses like 'Allah willed it' or 'It was my destiny'? How does this lesson's argument that Allah's rebuke only makes sense if you are the agent challenge that thinking? Will you consciously replace that excuse with taking full responsibility starting today?
?
The disbeliever in the passage could list twelve excuses if determinism were true. Think of the last time you blamed circumstances, another person, or even Allah for a wrong you did. Write down one specific excuse you have used. Now, in light of the verse 'How can you disbelieve?', are you willing to stand before Allah with that excuse? What will you do differently right now?
?
This lesson shows that a serious rebuke from Allah implies real choice. If you truly believed that every sin you commit is met with a divine rebuke that holds you accountable, how would your attitude toward minor sins change? Commit to one concrete action today that reflects this accountability.

The Mu'tazila's Argument from Quranic Rebuke
Mu'tazila present a verse and four reasons for disbelief originating from the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila used the verse 'How can you disbelieve?' to argue that disbelief originates from the servant, and their four rational proofs.

Core Idea
Quranic rebuke implies human free will.

The Mu'tazila cite Quranic verses that rebuke disbelievers, arguing that such rebuke would be unjust if Allah Himself created disbelief. This lesson presents their first verse and four reasons why it shows disbelief is from the servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the Mu'tazila's claim
Read the Arabic passage that presents the verse 'How can you disbelieve?' and the claim that it indicates disbelief is from the servant.
Notice how they immediately assert the verse supports their position.
2
Examine the four reasons
Go through each of the four numbered reasons (first, second, third, fourth). Understand the logic: if Allah created disbelief, divine rebuke would be as absurd as rebuking someone for turning black or white.
The fourth reason asks whether the verse is a genuine request for an answer or jest.
3
Identify the conclusion
See that the Mu'tazila conclude that the verse must be a genuine request for an answer, ruling out jest. This sets up the next lesson about the compellers' response.
The conclusion is that the verse is a direction of proof against the servant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث من استدلالات القوم : الآيات الدالة على أنه لا مانع لهم من الإيمان والطاعة ، ولا مجبر لهم على الكفر والمعصية . ولتذكر من هذا الجنس آيات :
أحدها : قوله تعالى : ( كيف تكفرون بالله ) ؟ قالوا : هذه الآية دالة على أن الكفر من قبل العبد . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : لو كان تعالى قد خلق الكفر فيهم ، لما جاز أن يقول : كيف تكفرون بالله ) على سبيل التوبيخ ، كما لا يجوز أن يقول : كيف تسودون وتبيضون وتصحون وتسقمون ؟ الثاني : إن كان خلقهم أولاً للشقاء وللنار ، أو ما أراد بخلقهم إلا الكفر والوقوع في النار فكيف يصح أن يقول موبخاً لهم : ( كيف تكفرون ) ؟ الثالث : كيف يليق بالحكيم أن يقول : كيف تكفرون ) حال ما يخلق الكفر فيهم ؟ الرابع : إنه تعالى إذا قال للكفار : كيف تكفرون ) فهل ذكر هذا الكلام توجيهاً للحجة على العبد ، وطلباً للجواب منه ، أو ذكره على سبيل الهزل والعبث ؟ والثاني لا يقول به مسلم. فبقي الأول : فنقول : لو كان موجد الكفر هو الله تعالى ، لكان للكافر أن يقول : عندي اثني عشر وجهاً من وجوه العذر . كل واحد منها مستقل بأن يكون جواباً عن هذا السؤال : فالأول : أنه يقول : يا إله الخلق خلقت الكفر في ، فكيف لا أكون كافراً. مع أني لا أقدر على دفع فعلك ؟ الثاني : أردت الكفر ، وأنا لا أقدر على دفع إرادتك . والثالث : علمت الكفر مني - وعند المجبرة : أن خلاف المعلوم ممتنع الوقوع والرابع : أجبرت على وقوع الكفر - وعند المجبرة ، خلاف خبر الله ممتنع الوقوع والخامس : إنك خلقت في قدرة لا تصلح إلا للكفر ، ومع حصول تلك القدرة يكون الكفر واجب الحصول . والسادس : إنك خلقت في إرادة جازمة مقتضية الحصول الكفر ، ومع حصول تلك الإرادة يكون الكفر واجب الحصول . وأيضاً : فكما حصلت هذه الأسباب الستة في جانب حصول الكفر ، فهي بأسرها مفقودة في جانب الإيمان . مع أنه يمتنع حصول الإيمان إلا عند حصول مجموعها . فثبت : أنه حصل لعدم الإيمان اثني عشر سببا . كل واحد منها مستقل بعدم الإيمان . فكيف يليق بالحكيم أن يقول مع هذه الموانع القاهرة : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا ) ؟ أو يقول : كيف تكفرون بالله ) اللهم إلا إذا قاله على سبيل الهزل . إلا أن على هذا التقدير يصير القرآن كتاب الهزل والسخرية
The third face of the arguments of the people: the verses indicating that there is no impediment for them to faith and obedience, and no compeller upon them to disbelief and disobedience. Let verses of this kind be mentioned: One of them: His statement, the Most High: (How can you disbelieve in Allah?) They said: This verse indicates that disbelief is from the servant. Its explanation is from aspects: First: If the Most High had created disbelief in them, He would not be allowed to say: How can you disbelieve in Allah? by way of rebuke, just as it is not allowed to say: How do you become black and white, and become healthy and sick? Second: If He had created them initially for misery and the Fire, or He did not intend by creating them except disbelief and falling into the Fire, then how is it valid for Him to say rebuking them: (How can you disbelieve?)? Third: How is it befitting of the Wise to say: How can you disbelieve? while He creates disbelief in them? Fourth: When the Most High says to the disbelievers: How can you disbelieve? – did He mention this speech as a direction of the proof against the servant, and a request for an answer from him, or did He mention it by way of jest and frivolity? The second is not held by any Muslim. So the first remains: We say: If the creator of disbelief were Allah the Most High, then the disbeliever would be able to say: I have twelve aspects of excuse. Each one of them is sufficient to be an answer to this question: The first: He says: O God of creation, You created disbelief in me, so how can I not be a disbeliever, given that I am unable to repel Your action? The second: You willed disbelief, and I am unable to repel Your will. The third: You knew disbelief from me – and according to the compellers (al-mujbira): that what contradicts what is known is impossible to occur. The fourth: You compelled me to the occurrence of disbelief – and according to the compellers: that contradicting the news of Allah is impossible to occur. The fifth: You created in me a power that is not fit except for disbelief, and with the existence of that power, disbelief is necessarily existent. The sixth: You created in me a decisive will necessitating the existence of disbelief, and with the existence of that will, disbelief is necessarily existent. Also: Just as these six causes have occurred in the side of the existence of disbelief, they are entirely absent in the side of faith, while it is impossible for faith to occur except when all of them exist. So it is established that for the non-existence of faith, twelve causes have occurred, each one of them sufficient for the non-existence of faith. So how is it befitting of the Wise to say, with these overwhelming obstacles: (And what would it have cost them if they had believed?) or to say: How can you disbelieve in Allah? – unless He said it by way of jest. Except that on this assumption, the Quran becomes a book of jest and mockery.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that certain Quranic verses prove human free will. The author presents the third argument of the Mu'tazila, who cite verses showing no compulsion. The first verse is "How can you disbelieve in Allah?" (al-Baqara 2:28). The Mu'tazila argue that this verse implies disbelief originates from the servant, because otherwise divine rebuke would be unjust. The author then provides four reasons why this verse would be problematic if Allah alone creates disbelief. He then presents a hypothetical counterargument from the perspective of the compellers (al-mujbira): if Allah were the sole creator of disbelief, the disbeliever could offer twelve excuses, each independently sufficient to invalidate the rebuke. The excuses are: (1) Allah created disbelief in him; (2) Allah willed his disbelief; (3) Allah knew his disbelief, and opposing what is known is impossible; (4) Allah compelled him; (5) Allah gave him a power only fit for disbelief; (6) Allah gave him a decisive will necessitating disbelief; (7-12) the same six causes are absent for faith, so faith is impossible. Therefore, with these obstacles, the Quranic rebuke would be meaningless unless it were jest, an impious conclusion. The author thus uses the compellers' own logic to reduce their position to absurdity.

التوبيخ

Checkpoint
According to the fourth reason, what would be the implication if the verse 'How can you disbelieve?' were said as a jest?
A) It would be a valid divine speech.
B) The Quran would become a book of jest and mockery.
C) The disbeliever would have no excuse.
D) The verse would still indicate free will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use Quranic rebuke to argue for human free will, with four reasons showing that divine creation of disbelief would render rebuke unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself falling into sin, do you truly feel that it was your own choice, or do you secretly blame it on Allah's decree? How honestly can you recite 'How can you disbelieve?' as a rebuke to yourself without hiding behind predestination?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that Allah's rebuke for disbelief only makes sense if the disbeliever is genuinely responsible. If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He questioned you about your faith, would you have an answer that comes from a real exercise of your will, or would you try to deflect responsibility?
?
If you believe that Allah's rebuke is a genuine call for you to respond, then what is stopping you today from answering that call with complete submission? Is it your own negligence, or are you still waiting for a divine push that never comes?

Divine Decree

The Twelve Excuses: Reductio of the Jabriyya
Hypothetical twelve excuses from disbelievers if Allah creates disbelief, leading to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hypothetical twelve excuses a disbeliever could offer if Allah were the sole creator of disbelief, and how this leads to the absurd conclusion that the Quran would be jest.

Core Idea
If Allah compels disbelief, then rebuke is meaningless.

The author now presents a reductio ad absurdum: if the compellers (Jabriyya) are right that Allah creates all actions, then disbelievers could offer twelve excuses, each sufficient to nullify the Quranic rebuke. This would force the conclusion that the Quran is jest—an impossibility for Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the hypothetical twelve excuses
Read the Arabic passage that presents the disbeliever's twelve excuses, starting with 'We say: If the creator of disbelief were Allah...'
Notice the structure: six causes on disbelief's side, and the same six absent on faith's side.
2
Enumerate the first six causes
Identify the six causes that make disbelief necessary: Allah created disbelief, willed it, knew it, compelled it, gave a power only for disbelief, and gave a decisive will for disbelief.
Each cause is claimed to be independently sufficient.
3
Understand the absence on faith's side
The same six causes are entirely absent for faith, making faith impossible. Thus there are twelve obstacles (six present for disbelief, six absent for faith).
The sum of twelve comes from 6 present + 6 absent.
4
Grasp the conclusion
With these overwhelming obstacles, Quranic verses like 'And what would it have cost them if they had believed?' or 'How can you disbelieve?' would be unjust unless they are jest—but jest makes the Quran a book of mockery, which is impious.
This reduces the Jabriyya position to absurdity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

The Second Verse and the Sixteen Obstacles
Second verse with reductio using sixteen obstacles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the second Quranic verse and a reductio ad absurdum argument to challenge the compeller's doctrine of divine prevention of faith.

Core Idea
The compellers' belief in sixteen obstacles makes God's questioning absurd.

This lesson examines the second Quranic verse presented by the author to refute the compellers. After explaining the verse's rhetorical nature, he draws a striking analogy and then lists sixteen obstacles that the compellers claim prevent faith. The argument culminates in a powerful reductio: if God truly prevents faith through all these means, His questioning of disbelief becomes meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its explanation
Read the Arabic verse 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' and note that it is a rhetorical denial (inkar). Understand that the verse implies blame on those who do not believe.
The verse is from Surah al-Kahf (18:55).
2
Understand the analogy of the confined servant
The author draws an analogy: if a master confines his servant so he cannot move, then asks why he didn't attend to his needs, that speech would be frivolous. This illustrates the absurdity of God questioning disbelief if He Himself prevents it.
The analogy is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
List the sixteen obstacles
The compellers (al-mujbira) claim sixteen obstacles that prevent faith: sealing, stamping, hardness, covering, heaviness, deafness, muteness, blindness, barrier, misguidance, leading astray, restraint, turning away, deceit, gradual entrapment, and deception. Each is independently sufficient to prevent faith.
These are not affirmed by the author; they are attributed to the compellers.
4
Grasp the overall argument
With all these obstacles present, the author argues it is unfitting for the All-Wise God to ask 'What prevented the people from believing?' The existence of twelve excuses further strengthens the point. The passage ends by introducing a third verse.
The third verse is not fully developed in this excerpt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا إذ جاءهم الهدى ) ؟ وهو إنكار بلفظ الاستفهام . ومعلوم : أن رجلاً لو جلس في موضع ، بحيث لا يمكنه الخروج، وقال له :مولاه : ما منعك عن التصرف في حوائجي ، كان ذلك الكلام عبثاً . وأيضاً : المجبرة يقولون : إن الختم والطبع [ والقسوة ] والغشاوة والوقر والصم والبكم والعمى والسد والإغواء والإضلال والإرانة والصرف والمكر والاستدراج والخداع . وكل واحد من هذه الأمور الستة عشر : سببه مستقل بالمنع من الإيمان . فمع قيام هذه الموانع بأسرها كيف [ يليق ] بالحكيم أن يقول : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ؟ ) وأيضاً : الأعذار الاثني عشر قائمة ، ومع حصولها ، كيف يليق بالحكيم أن يقول : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ) وثالثها : قوله تعالى لإبليس ( ما منعك أن تجد ( ؟ ) وقول موسى
And the second of them: His statement, the Most High: (And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?) And it is a denial by way of interrogation. And it is known: If a man were to sit in a place such that he cannot leave, and his master said to him: What prevented you from attending to my needs? that speech would be frivolous. Also, the compellers (al-mujbira) say: Indeed, the sealing and the stamping [and the hardness], and the covering, and the heaviness, and the deafness, and the muteness, and the blindness, and the barrier, and the misguidance, and the leading astray, and the restraint, and the turning away, and the deceit, and the gradual entrapment, and the deception. And each one of these sixteen matters: its cause is independently sufficient to prevent faith. So with the existence of all these obstacles, how is it befitting of the Wise to say: (And what prevented the people from believing?) Also, the twelve excuses are present, and with their existence, how is it befitting of the Wise to say: (And what prevented the people from believing?) And the third of them: His statement, the Most High, to Iblis: (What prevented you from prostrating?) and the statement of Moses...
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by introducing the second Quranic verse: 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' (al-Kahf 18:55). The author explains that this is a rhetorical denial (inkar) implying blame. He then draws an analogy: if a master confines his servant to a place and then asks why he didn't attend to his needs, that would be absurd. Similarly, the compellers believe that Allah actively prevents faith through sixteen specific obstacles: sealing, stamping, hardness, covering, heaviness, deafness, muteness, blindness, barrier, misguidance, leading astray, restraint, turning away, deceit, gradual entrapment, and deception. Each of these is independently sufficient to prevent faith. Therefore, with all these obstacles in place, it would be unfitting for the All-Wise to ask why people do not believe. The twelve excuses from the previous argument also apply. The passage then mentions the third verse (about Iblis and Moses) but does not develop it fully; the text breaks off. The overall point is that if divine prevention is real, divine questioning becomes meaningless.

Key Terms
الآية
الموانع الستة عشر
الختم
الطبع
القسوة
المجبرة
الإستفهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is affirming the existence of sixteen obstacles that prevent faith.
Correction: The author lists the obstacles only as part of a reductio ad absurdum against the compellers; he does not endorse them.

Checkpoint
How many specific obstacles do the compellers (al-mujbira) claim prevent faith, according to this passage?
A) Twelve
B) Sixteen
C) Six
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The second verse shows that if God actively prevents faith, His questioning of disbelief is logically inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself neglecting your prayers or disobeying Allah, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It's because Allah hasn't guided me' or 'My heart is hard'? How does this lesson expose that excuse as absurd if you have the ability to choose? What specific step will you take today to act against that excuse?
?
The master's question was frivolous because he had already prevented the servant. In your life, are there any areas where you have created your own 'obstacles'—procrastination, distractions, or sins—and then complained that Allah is not helping you? Write down one such area and commit to removing that obstacle by your own will, trusting that Allah does not prevent you.
?
If Allah, the All-Wise, asks you on the Day of Judgment why you did not believe or obey, will you have any real excuse? Consider that all obstacles come from your own choices. What is the one thing you will change starting today so that your answer is not silence?

The Twelve Causes: Why God's Reproach Would Be Absurd
Sanusi presents the twelve causes argument against Jabari determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the concept of twelve causes to show that Jabari determinism makes God's questioning in the Quran absurd.

Core Idea
If God creates all obstacles to faith, His reproach becomes meaningless.

The author begins by summarizing the Jabari claim that God creates all necessary causes for disbelief and removes them for faith, resulting in twelve independent reasons why faith does not occur. He then asks: with all these obstacles, how can God's reproachful questions in the Quran be taken seriously?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Pay attention to the enumeration of causes.
2
Identify the twelve causes
The passage presents six causes present for disbelief and six absent for faith. Find each and notice how the author concludes twelve independent reasons.
3
Reflect on the analogy
The author gives the example of a master and servant. Understand why this analogy shows the absurdity of God's reproach if He is the cause of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصلح إلا للكفر ، ومع حصول تلك القدرة يكون الكفر واجب الحصول . والسادس : إنك خلقت في إرادة جازمة مقتضية الحصول الكفر ، ومع حصول تلك الإرادة يكون الكفر واجب الحصول . وأيضاً : فكما حصلت هذه الأسباب الستة في جانب حصول الكفر ، فهي بأسرها مفقودة في جانب الإيمان . مع أنه يمتنع حصول الإيمان إلا عند حصول مجموعها . فثبت : أنه حصل لعدم الإيمان اثني عشر سببا . كل واحد منها مستقل بعدم الإيمان . فكيف يليق بالحكيم أن يقول مع هذه الموانع القاهرة : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا ) ؟ أو يقول : كيف تكفرون بالله ) ؟ اللهم إلا إذا قاله على سبيل الهذل . إلا أن على هذا التقدير يصير القرآن كتاب الهزل والسخرية وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا إذ جاءهم الهدى ) ؟ وهو إنكار بلفظ الاستفهام . ومعلوم : أن رجلاً لو جلس في موضع ، بحيث لا يمكنه الخروج، وقال له :مولاه : ما منعك عن التصرف في حوائجي ، كان ذلك الكلام عبثاً . وأيضاً : المجبرة يقولون : إن الختم والطبع [ والقسوة ] والغشاوة والوقر والصم والبكم والعمى والسد والإغواء والإضلال والإرانة والصرف والمكر والاستدراج والخداع . وكل واحد من هذه الأمور الستة عشر : سببه مستقل بالمنع من الإيمان . فمع قيام هذه الموانع بأسرها كيف [ يليق ] بالحكيم أن يقول : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ؟ ) وأيضاً : الأعذار الاثني عشر قائمة ، ومع حصولها ، كيف يليق بالحكيم أن يقول : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ) وثالثها : قوله تعالى لإبليس ( ما منعك أن تجد (؟) ) وقول موسى عليه السلام لأخيه : ( ما منعك إذ رأيتهم ضلوا ) ؟ [ وقوله ] فيا لهم لا يؤمنون ) ؟ و - فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين ) - ( عفا الله عنك . لم أذنت لهم ) - لم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ) - ( لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل ) ؟ - لم تصدون عن سبيل الله ) ؟ وقال : الصاحب بن عباد في فصل له في هذا الباب : كيف يأمر بالإيمان ولم يرده ، وينهي عن الكفر ويريده ، ويعاقب على الباطل ويقدره ، وكيف يصرفه عن الإيمان ، ثم يقول : (أنى تصرفون ) ويخلق فيهم الإفك ، ثم يقول : أنى يؤفكون ) ؟ وينشأ فيهم الكفر ، ثم يقول : لم تكفرون ) ؟ ويخلق فيهم لبس الحق بالباطل [ ثم يقول : ( لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل )] ويصدهم عن سبيل الله ، ثم يقول : ( لم تصدون عن سبيل الله ) وحال بينهم وبين الإيمان ، ثم يقول : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا ) وذهب بهم عن الرشد ثم قال : ( فأين تذهبون ) وأضلهم عن الدين ، حتى أعرضوا ، ثم قال : فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين
It is not suitable except for disbelief, and with the occurrence of that power (qudra), disbelief becomes necessarily occurring. The sixth: You created in him a decisive will (irada jazima) necessitating the occurrence of disbelief, and with the occurrence of that will, disbelief becomes necessarily occurring. Also: Just as these six causes (asbab) occurred on the side of the occurrence of disbelief, they are entirely absent on the side of faith. While faith cannot occur except when all of them are present. So it is established that for the non-occurrence of faith there are twelve causes, each one independently sufficient for the non-occurrence of faith. So how is it fitting for the Wise (al-Hakim) to say with these overwhelming obstacles: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' or to say: 'How do you disbelieve in Allah?' Unless He says it as jest. But on this assumption, the Quran becomes a book of jest and mockery. The second: His saying: 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' It is a denial by way of interrogation. It is known that if a man sat in a place such that he could not leave, and his master said to him: 'What prevented you from attending to my needs?' that speech would be absurd. Also: The determinists (al-mujbira) say: The sealing (khatm), stamping (tab'), hardness (qaswa), covering (ghishawa), deafness (waqr), dumbness (summ), blindness, obstruction (sadd), misleading (ighwa'), leading astray (idlal), aversion (irana), turning away (sarf), deception (makr), gradual punishment (istidraj), and delusion (khada') — each one of these sixteen things: its cause is independently sufficient to prevent faith. So with all these obstacles present, how is it fitting for the Wise to say: 'And what prevented the people from believing?' And also: The twelve excuses are present, and with them present, how is it fitting for the Wise to say: 'And what prevented the people from believing?' The third: His saying to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating?' and the saying of Moses to his brother: 'What prevented you when you saw them go astray?' and [His saying]: 'What is the matter with them that they do not believe?' and 'Why are they turning away from the reminder?' and 'May Allah pardon you, why did you give them permission?' and 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' and 'Why do you clothe the truth with falsehood?' and 'Why do you turn people away from the way of Allah?' And al-Sahib ibn Abbad said in a section on this topic: How does He command faith and not will it, and forbid disbelief and will it, and punish falsehood and decree it? And how does He turn them away from faith, then say: 'How are you turned away?' and He creates in them falsehood, then say: 'How are they liars?' and He originates in them disbelief, then say: 'Why do you disbelieve?' and He creates in them the clothing of truth with falsehood, then say: 'Why do you clothe truth with falsehood?' and He hinders them from the path of Allah, then say: 'Why do you hinder from the path of Allah?' and He interposes between them and faith, then says: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' and He takes them away from guidance, then says: 'Where are you going?' and He leads them astray from the religion until they turn away, then says: 'Why are they turning away from the reminder?'
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabari (determinist) position that God creates all human actions, including disbelief, and thus humans have no choice. The author argues that if God truly creates all obstacles to faith, then His reproachful questions in the Quran become absurd. The argument proceeds by first summarizing the Jabari claim: for disbelief, there are six causes (power, will, etc.) all created by God; for faith, these same six causes are absent, making faith impossible. Thus there are twelve reasons (six present for disbelief, six absent for faith) each independently sufficient for the non-occurrence of faith. Given these overwhelming obstacles, the author asks how a wise being could say 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' or 'How do you disbelieve in Allah?' unless as jest, which would reduce the Quran to mockery. Secondly, the author examines the verse 'And what prevented the people from believing...?' If, as the Jabari claim, God has blocked all means of guidance, then asking 'what prevented them?' is like a master who imprisons his servant and then asks why he didn't attend to his needs—it is meaningless. The author lists sixteen divine acts (sealing, stamping, etc.) that the Jabari claim are independent preventers of faith. With all these barriers, how can God ask 'What prevented them?' Thirdly, the author cites several Quranic reproaches (to Iblis, to Moses' brother, and various rhetorical questions) that imply blame for disbelief or neglect. If God is the sole cause of disbelief, these reproaches are unjust. Finally, the author quotes al-Sahib ibn Abbad, who poetically highlights the contradiction: God commands faith but does not will it; forbids disbelief but wills it; punishes falsehood but decrees it; and so on. Each pair opposes God's action to His word. The overall point is that the Jabari theology undermines the Quran's moral coherence and turns it into a book of contradictions.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
الإيمان
الكفر
الجبر
الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabari believe that God's creation of all causes negates human responsibility, but they still accept God's reproaches as meaningful.
Correction: The passage shows that if God creates all causes, then His reproaches become absurd, because He is blaming people for what He Himself caused.

Checkpoint
How many independent reasons does the author claim prevent the occurrence of faith?
A) Six
B) Twelve
C) Sixteen
D) Eighteen

Key Takeaway
The twelve-causes argument demonstrates that Jabari theology renders Quranic reproaches nonsensical, revealing a deep contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sixteen Divine Acts: Are They Real Obstacles to Faith?
Sanusi enumerates sixteen divine obstacles and argues they make God's reproach meaningless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's list of sixteen divine acts that the Jabari claim prevent faith, and why this makes God's question 'What prevented them?' absurd.

Core Idea
If God seals hearts, blinds eyes, and misleads, then asking 'What prevented you?' is absurd.

The author continues by citing the Jabari belief that God actively prevents faith through sixteen specific acts mentioned in the Quran. He argues that with all these barriers in place, God's question 'What prevented the people from believing?' becomes a contradiction. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of sixteen obstacles
Read the passage and identify the sixteen terms. They are all Quranic descriptions of divine action.
These include khatm (sealing), tab' (stamping), ghishawa (covering), etc.
2
Understand the Jabari use of these terms
The Jabari claim each of these is an independent cause that prevents faith. Notice how the author lists them.
3
Connect to the reproach question
With all sixteen obstacles present, the author asks how God can then say 'What prevented them?' This shows the contradiction.

Quranic Reproaches Contradict Jabari Determinism
Sanusi cites Quranic reproaches and Ibn Abbad's summary to refute Jabari determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses that reproach people for disbelief, including the saying of Ibn Abbad, reveal the inconsistency of Jabari determinism.

Core Idea
God's reproaches in the Quran are meaningless if He alone causes the blameworthy actions.

The author concludes by citing several Quranic reproaches, such as to Iblis and to the disbelievers, and then quotes al-Sahib ibn Abbad who poetically lists the contradictions between God's commands and His supposed actions. This lesson explores how these reproaches undermine the Jabari claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic reproaches
The author lists several verses where God or prophets reproach others. Read each one and note the implied blame.
2
Understand Ibn Abbad's argument
Ibn Abbad presents a series of rhetorical questions that contrast God's commands with His supposed actions under Jabari theology.
Notice the pattern: God commands X but does not will it, or forbids Y but wills it.
3
Synthesize the overall refutation
Connect this lesson to the previous two. All together, the author shows that Jabari determinism cannot account for the moral coherence of the Quran.

فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين ) - ( عفا الله عنك . لم أذنت لهم ) - لم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ) - ( لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل ) ؟ - لم تصدون عن سبيل الله ) ؟ وقال : الصاحب بن عباد في فصل له في هذا الباب : كيف يأمر بالإيمان ولم يرده ، وينهي عن الكفر ويريده ، ويعاقب على الباطل ويقدره ، وكيف يصرفه عن الإيمان ، ثم يقول : (أنى تصرفون ) ويخلق فيهم الإفك ، ثم يقول : أنى يؤفكون ) ؟ وينشأ فيهم الكفر ، ثم يقول : لم تكفرون ) ؟ ويخلق فيهم لبس الحق بالباطل [ ثم يقول : ( لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل )] ويصدهم عن سبيل الله ، ثم يقول : ( لم تصدون عن سبيل الله ) وحال بينهم وبين الإيمان ، ثم يقول : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا ) وذهب بهم عن الرشد ثم قال : ( فأين تذهبون ) وأضلهم عن الدين ، حتى أعرضوا ، ثم قال : فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين
It is not suitable except for disbelief, and with the occurrence of that power (qudra), disbelief becomes necessarily occurring. The sixth: You created in him a decisive will (irada jazima) necessitating the occurrence of disbelief, and with the occurrence of that will, disbelief becomes necessarily occurring. Also: Just as these six causes (asbab) occurred on the side of the occurrence of disbelief, they are entirely absent on the side of faith. While faith cannot occur except when all of them are present. So it is established that for the non-occurrence of faith there are twelve causes, each one independently sufficient for the non-occurrence of faith. So how is it fitting for the Wise (al-Hakim) to say with these overwhelming obstacles: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' or to say: 'How do you disbelieve in Allah?' Unless He says it as jest. But on this assumption, the Quran becomes a book of jest and mockery. The second: His saying: 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' It is a denial by way of interrogation. It is known that if a man sat in a place such that he could not leave, and his master said to him: 'What prevented you from attending to my needs?' that speech would be absurd. Also: The determinists (al-mujbira) say: The sealing (khatm), stamping (tab'), hardness (qaswa), covering (ghishawa), deafness (waqr), dumbness (summ), blindness, obstruction (sadd), misleading (ighwa'), leading astray (idlal), aversion (irana), turning away (sarf), deception (makr), gradual punishment (istidraj), and delusion (khada') — each one of these sixteen things: its cause is independently sufficient to prevent faith. So with all these obstacles present, how is it fitting for the Wise to say: 'And what prevented the people from believing?' And also: The twelve excuses are present, and with them present, how is it fitting for the Wise to say: 'And what prevented the people from believing?' The third: His saying to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating?' and the saying of Moses to his brother: 'What prevented you when you saw them go astray?' and [His saying]: 'What is the matter with them that they do not believe?' and 'Why are they turning away from the reminder?' and 'May Allah pardon you, why did you give them permission?' and 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' and 'Why do you clothe the truth with falsehood?' and 'Why do you turn people away from the way of Allah?' And al-Sahib ibn Abbad said in a section on this topic: How does He command faith and not will it, and forbid disbelief and will it, and punish falsehood and decree it? And how does He turn them away from faith, then say: 'How are you turned away?' and He creates in them falsehood, then say: 'How are they liars?' and He originates in them disbelief, then say: 'Why do you disbelieve?' and He creates in them the clothing of truth with falsehood, then say: 'Why do you clothe truth with falsehood?' and He hinders them from the path of Allah, then say: 'Why do you hinder from the path of Allah?' and He interposes between them and faith, then says: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' and He takes them away from guidance, then says: 'Where are you going?' and He leads them astray from the religion until they turn away, then says: 'Why are they turning away from the reminder?'
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabari (determinist) position that God creates all human actions, including disbelief, and thus humans have no choice. The author argues that if God truly creates all obstacles to faith, then His reproachful questions in the Quran become absurd. The argument proceeds by first summarizing the Jabari claim: for disbelief, there are six causes (power, will, etc.) all created by God; for faith, these same six causes are absent, making faith impossible. Thus there are twelve reasons (six present for disbelief, six absent for faith) each independently sufficient for the non-occurrence of faith. Given these overwhelming obstacles, the author asks how a wise being could say 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' or 'How do you disbelieve in Allah?' unless as jest, which would reduce the Quran to mockery. Secondly, the author examines the verse 'And what prevented the people from believing...?' If, as the Jabari claim, God has blocked all means of guidance, then asking 'what prevented them?' is like a master who imprisons his servant and then asks why he didn't attend to his needs—it is meaningless. The author lists sixteen divine acts (sealing, stamping, etc.) that the Jabari claim are independent preventers of faith. With all these barriers, how can God ask 'What prevented them?' Thirdly, the author cites several Quranic reproaches (to Iblis, to Moses' brother, and various rhetorical questions) that imply blame for disbelief or neglect. If God is the sole cause of disbelief, these reproaches are unjust. Finally, the author quotes al-Sahib ibn Abbad, who poetically highlights the contradiction: God commands faith but does not will it; forbids disbelief but wills it; punishes falsehood but decrees it; and so on. Each pair opposes God's action to His word. The overall point is that the Jabari theology undermines the Quran's moral coherence and turns it into a book of contradictions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabari believe that God can rightfully reproach people even if He determines their actions, because He is the sovereign.
Correction: The author shows that reproach implies blame, which is meaningless if the blamed action is determined by the one who reproaches. The Quran's consistent use of reproach assumes human free will.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sahib ibn Abbad, which of the following is a contradiction in Jabari theology?
A) God commands faith and wills it
B) God forbids disbelief and does not will it
C) God punishes falsehood but decrees it
D) God creates guidance and asks why people reject it

Key Takeaway
Quranic reproaches and Ibn Abbad's analysis demonstrate that Jabari determinism is incompatible with the moral and rational structure of the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic reproaches like 'Why do you disbelieve?' do you feel the weight of your own accountability, or do you instinctively think 'it was written'? Today, what is one area of your life where you are using divine decree as an excuse to avoid obedience?
?
Ibn Abbad's argument shows that God's speech and actions are in perfect harmony. If you find yourself assuming that God's decree exempts you from hearing His call, where is the contradiction in your own heart? What reproach from the Quran are you avoiding right now by blaming destiny?
?
God asks 'What harm would it do them if they believed?' – He highlights the ease of faith. What is stopping you from that small step today? Is it fear, pride, or a false belief that God has already written your end? What will you do in the next hour to prove that you are not a Jabari in practice?

Divine Decree

Al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad's Objection on Divine Decree
Al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad enumerates Quranic contradictions between divine decree and human reproach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological objection raised by Al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad regarding the apparent contradiction between divine decree and human accountability in the Quran.

Core Idea
Divine commands and decrees seem to conflict—how can Allah punish what He Himself creates?

Al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad presents a powerful objection to the Ash'ari position on divine decree. He points to a series of Quranic verses where Allah reproaches humans for actions that, according to the doctrine, Allah Himself creates and wills. This passage challenges the coherence of holding humans accountable for divinely decreed acts. Understanding this objection is key to grappling with the problem of qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the rhetorical structure. Notice how each statement pairs a divine action with a Quranic rebuke.
Pay attention to the repetitive pattern: Allah does X, then says Y.
2
Identify the pattern
List the pairs: e.g., Allah commands faith but does not will it; Allah forbids disbelief but wills it. Recognize that each pair contrasts Allah's decree with His command or reproach.
There are about eleven such pairs in the passage.
3
Consider the theological problem
Ask yourself: If Allah creates all actions, including disbelief and sin, how can He justly punish people for those same actions? This is the core of the objection.
This objection challenges the consistency of divine justice and omnipotence.
4
Reflect on possible responses
Think about how a theologian might answer. Common responses include distinguishing between Allah's creative will (irāda takwīniyya) and legislative will (irāda shar‘iyya), or the concept of acquisition (kasb).
The passage itself does not provide the answer; it only states the problem.

Al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad said in a chapter of his on this topic: How does He command faith while not willing it, and forbid disbelief while willing it, and punish for falsehood while decreeing it, and how does He turn them away from faith, then say: 'Where are you going?' and creates in them falsehood, then says: 'How are they perverted?' and originates in them disbelief, then says: 'Why do you disbelieve?' and creates in them the mixing of truth with falsehood, then says: 'Why do you mix truth with falsehood?' and bars them from the path of Allah, then says: 'Why do you bar from the path of Allah?' and He interposes between them and faith, then says: 'What would it harm them if they believed?' and He takes them away from guidance, then says: 'Where are you going?' and He leads them astray from religion until they turn away, then says: 'What is the matter with them that they turn away from the reminder?'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a statement by al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad that raises a theological objection about divine decree and human responsibility. He presents a series of rhetorical questions highlighting the apparent contradiction: Allah commands faith but does not will it for some, forbids disbelief but wills it, punishes falsehood while decreeing it, and then criticizes people for actions He Himself creates. Each question pairs a divine action (e.g., creating disbelief) with a Quranic verse that reproaches humans for that same action (e.g., 'Why do you disbelieve?'). The point is to challenge the coherence of holding humans accountable for acts that are divinely decreed. This objection is a classic problem in kalam, especially concerning qadar (divine decree) and human free will.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this objection denies divine justice entirely.
Correction: It is actually a challenge to reconcile divine decree with human accountability, not a denial of justice.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad's objection highlights the apparent contradiction between divine decree and Quranic reproaches, a central problem in the theology of qadar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Logic of Divine Help and Mutual Help
Verses and objection about help, with an Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school explains the rationality of commands to seek God's help and to help each other within a framework that affirms both divine sovereignty and human agency.

Core Idea
Human ability is real but incomplete; help consists in removing obstacles and pooling capacities.

This passage tackles a critical objection against human agency: if God is the sole creator of all actions, why would He command believers to seek His help or to help one another? The author cites Quranic verses, then provides an Ash'ari response that preserves human responsibility while maintaining God's ultimate role. By understanding the necessary conditions for an action—ability, motive, and removal of obstacles—the commands become perfectly rational.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic commands
Read the verses about seeking help from God and mutual help. Notice that they assume some human capacity but also dependence on others.
The verses are: 'You alone we seek help,' 'Seek refuge with Allah,' 'And cooperate in righteousness.'
2
Understand the objection
The objection states: if humans have no genuine ability, commanding them to seek help is futile. Grasp why this objection is powerful.
The key phrase: 'If the servant were not capable, commanding him to seek help would be futile.'
3
Follow the answer's structure
The author answers by defining the necessary condition for action (ability + motive) and explaining how God's help removes obstacles. Then he addresses mutual help as combining multiple abilities.
Note the terms: mallzum (necessary condition), qudra (ability), da'i (motive), mawani' (obstacles).
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this model makes sense of both commands: God's help removes internal/external obstacles; mutual help supplies needed power from others.
The answer concludes: 'So the problem is resolved.'

التنوع الرابع
من وجوه استدلالات المعنية بالقيان [؟]
