Objection and Answer on Divine Reminder and Determinism
Author presents an objection from Qur'an 35:37 and gives a dialectical answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes an objection based on Q. 35:37 by showing that the objector's own reasoning leads to coercion (jabr) and the impossibility of contradicting God's foreknowledge.

Core Idea
Even the objector's argument leads to the very determinism they reject.

This passage presents a classical kalām objection-and-response. The objector uses a Qur'anic verse (35:37) to argue that God's rebuke of disbelievers would be unfair if faith is created by God. The author turns the objection back by showing that the objector's own assumptions lead to coercion (jabr) and impossibility of contradicting God's knowledge. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its scriptural basis
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The objector cites Q. 35:37 and argues that God's argument would be invalid if faith were created by God.
Notice how the objector uses the verse to challenge divine justice.
2
Identify the objector's inference
The objector claims that if faith depends on God's creation, then disbelievers could respond that God created disbelief in them. They could say 'If You had created faith, we would have believed.'
This is a common kalām objection against predestinarian views.
3
Examine the author's answer
The author argues that this objection is itself problematic. Because the occurrence of any act depends on God's creation of power (qudra) and motive (dā'ī), which entails jabr (coercion). Also, it is impossible for anything to contradict God's foreknowledge.
The author uses the objector's own framework to show inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the broader kalām context
Consider how this response fits into debates on free will and predestination. The author does not defend jabr outright but uses it dialectically to refute the objector.
This is a typical Ash'ari or Maturidi approach using the opponent's assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس عشر القيم
قوله تعالى : ( أو لم نعمركم ما يتذكر فيه من تذكر ، وجاءكم النذير )
ووجه الاستدلال : إنه تعالى احتج عليهم بالتمكن والإقدار ، والعمر الطويل ، والمهلة في أوقات التكليف ، وإزاحة الأعذار ببعثة الرسل . فلو كان الإيمان والتذكر موقوفين على خلق الله ، لكان لهم أن يقولوا : الهتنا قد فعلت فينا الكفر ، والعدول عن طاعتك ، وكنا عاجزين عن دفع ما خلقت ، ولم تخلق فينا الإيمان . ولو خلقت الإيمان فينا لكنا مؤمنين بك . قالوا : فكيف يعقل أن يحتج الله سبحانه عليهم بما لا حجة له فيه ، مع أنه قال : ( قل فلله الحجة البالغة ) ؟ وليس لهم أن يقولوا : المناظرة مع الله لا تجوز . لأن هذا الكلام قد سبق جوابه .
والجواب : إن كل ذلك مشكل عليهم ، بما أن حصول الفعل موقوف على خلق القدرة والداعي وذلك يوجب الجبر ، ويشكل أيضاً . بما أن خلاف معلوم الله محال الوقوع .
The fifteenth type: al-Qayyim. His statement, the Exalted: 'Or did We not give you long life enough for him who reflects to reflect therein, and the warner came to you?' (35:37). And the point of inference: He, the Exalted, argued against them by (giving them) ability and empowerment, long life, respite in times of obligation, and removing excuses by sending messengers. So if faith and reflection were dependent on God's creation, they could say: Our god has caused disbelief in us and turning away from Your obedience, and we were unable to repel what You created, and You did not create faith in us. And if You had created faith in us, we would have been believers in You. They said: How can it be reasonable that God, glorified, argues against them with something in which He has no proof, especially since He said: 'Say, to God belongs the conclusive argument'? And they cannot say: Debate with God is not permissible, because this statement has been answered before. The answer: All of that is problematic for them, because the occurrence of an act depends on the creation of power and the motive, and that necessitates coercion (jabr), and it is also problematic because that which contradicts God's knowledge is impossible to occur.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within a kalam discussion on divine decree and human responsibility. The objector cites the Qur'anic verse (35:37) where God rebukes the people of the Fire, noting that they were given long life and a warner. The objector argues that if faith and reflection were created by God, then God's rebuke would be invalid because the disbelievers could claim that God caused their disbelief and did not create faith in them. They could say that if God had created faith, they would have believed. Thus, the objector concludes that God's argument would lack proof, contradicting His own statement that the conclusive argument belongs to Him. The author immediately notes that the objection that debating God is not permissible has already been answered. The author's answer is that this very objection is problematic for the objectors themselves. The reason is that the occurrence of an act (like faith or disbelief) depends on the creation of both the power (qudra) and the motive (da'ī) by God. This entails coercion (jabr), which is a problem for the objector's position. Furthermore, it is impossible for anything to occur that contradicts God's foreknowledge. Thus, the objector's argument is turned back, showing that their own position leads to the same or worse difficulties.

جبر (jabr)
خلق القدرة والداعي (creation of power and motive)
معلوم الله (God's knowledge)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author in this passage is defending determinism (jabr) as his own position.
Correction: The author is not endorsing jabr; he is using it dialectically to refute the objector by showing that the objector's own assumptions lead to jabr.

C) A proof for the validity of the objection

Key Takeaway
The objector's argument against determinism, based on the verse about divine reminder, actually leads to the very determinism it seeks to refute.

Refutation of Jabriyya's Quranic Proof
Author responds to Jabriyya proof from Quran 38:58-61.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Jabriyya's proof from Quran 38:58-61 and shows that the same difficulty applies to the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The same objection used against the Jabriyya also challenges the Mu'tazila.

The Jabriyya cite a Quranic verse where the inhabitants of Hell curse those who brought them there, arguing this shows God is the cause of their punishment. The author, however, points out that this line of reasoning equally applies to the Mu'tazila's own positions on motive and knowledge. In this lesson, we will examine the verse, the Jabriyya argument, and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the objection and answer.
2
Identify the Jabriyya argument
Notice how the Jabriyya use the verse to claim that God is the one who brought the punishment upon people.
The verse itself is quoted; the Jabriyya infer that 'He' refers to God.
3
Understand the author's response
The author says 'consider what follows' from this claim, implying it leads to problematic implications. Then he gives the answer: the same objection applies to the Mu'tazila on the issues of da'i and ilm.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Think about why this response is effective: it shifts the burden back to the Mu'tazila, showing the verse does not uniquely support either school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسكوا بقوله تعالى : ( وآخر من شكله أزواج . هذا فوج مقتحم معكم لا مرحباً بهم . إنهم صالوا النار . قالوا : بل أنتم لا مرحباً بكم : أنتم قدمتموه لنا . فبئس القرار . قالوا : ربنا من قدم لنا هذا ، فزده عذاباً ضعفاً في النار ) قالوا : على مذهب المجبرة : إنه تعالى هو الذي قدم لهم . فانظر ما يلزم على قول المجبرة في هذا الموضوع. والجواب : إن هذا أيضاً وارد على المعتزلة مسألة الداعي ، ومسألة العلم . والله أعلم .
They adhered to His saying, the Most High: (And other [punishments] of similar kind in pairs. [It will be said], 'This is a company bursting in with you; no welcome for them. Indeed, they will burn in the Fire.' They will say, 'Rather, you [are the ones] no welcome for you. You brought it upon us. And wretched is the resting place.' They will say, 'Our Lord, whoever brought this upon us - increase for him double punishment in the Fire.') They said: According to the school of the Jabriyya: Indeed, He, the Most High, is the One who brought it upon them. So consider what follows from the statement of the Jabriyya on this matter. And the answer: This also applies to the Mu'tazila regarding the issue of the motive (al-da'i) and the issue of knowledge (al-ilm). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer unit. The objectors—apparently proponents of the Jabriyya (compulsionist) school—adduce a Quranic verse (Surah Sad 38:58-61) where the inhabitants of Hell curse those who brought them to Hell, and they ask God to double the punishment of those who led them astray. The Jabriyya argue that this verse implies that God Himself is the one who brought them to Hell, since He is the ultimate creator of all actions. The author then says: 'Consider what follows from the statement of the Jabriyya on this matter'—that is, note the problematic implications of their claim. The answer is that this same objection (i.e., the apparent implication that God is the cause of evil) also applies to the Mu'tazila on two issues: the issue of the motive (al-da'i) and the issue of knowledge (al-ilm). In other words, the Mu'tazila also face a similar difficulty when explaining how human free will interacts with divine knowledge and the motive for action. Thus, the verse does not uniquely refute the Jabriyya; it poses a challenge that both schools must address. The passage concludes with 'And God knows best,' acknowledging that the full resolution is with God.

Refutation using the verse of Samiri
The author refutes the claim that misguidance is from servants using the verse 'And Samiri led them astray'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'And Samiri led them astray' refutes the claim that actions are exclusively from servants, by showing that misguidance can be attributed to a human without contradicting divine sovereignty.

Core Idea
The Samiri verse proves that misguidance is genuinely from the servant, not just a divine creation.

This passage presents a key argument in the qadar debate. Opponents claim that universal testimony supports human agency. The author counters by citing a Qur'anic verse where misguidance is attributed to Samiri, not directly to God. This refutation hinges on the attribution of action to the servant while maintaining God's ultimate sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' position
The opponents argue that God, prophets, believers, and even Iblis have testified that actions truly issue from human servants. This sets up the challenge.
Note the cumulative nature of their argument.
2
Analyze the author's proof
The author cites the verse 'And Samiri led them astray' and argues that if God were the sole creator of misguidance, attributing it to Samiri would be false. Therefore, misguidance must be genuinely from Samiri.
3
Address the variant reading
Some read the verse as 'And Samiri led them astray' meaning 'the most astray among them is Samiri'. The author maintains that their preferred reading is authentic and sufficient. The variant does not contradict because being astray does not preclude being a misleader.
This shows the author's careful engagement with different qira'at.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This argument shows that human actions can be genuinely attributed to the servant while still acknowledging Allah as the ultimate creator. It supports the Ash'ari position on kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثامن عشر القيم
قالوا : إله العالم شهد بأن هذه الأفعال صدرت عن العباد . والأنبياء اعترفوا به ، والمؤمنون أقروا به ، وإبليس اللعين اعترف به . فمن أنكر ذلك فقد خالف [ كل (۱) ] هؤلاء . أما بيان أن الله تعالى شهد به ، ففي آيات :
( وأضلهم السامري (۲) ) وهذا تصريح بأن ذلك الإضلال من السامري ، ولو كان خالق الضلال هو ا تعالى لكانت إضافة الإضلال إلى السامري كذباً . ولا يقال : قراءة بعضهم : ( وأضلهم السامري (٣) ) أي : أشدهم ضلالاً هو السامري - لأنا نقول : القراءة التي تمسكنا بها حقة صحيحة بالإتفاق وهي تدل على قولنا . فقد حصل المقصود . وأما القراءة التي ذكرتم فإنها لا تنافي ما ذكرناه لأن كون السامري ضالاً ، لا ينافي كونه مضلاً
The eighteenth type: the values. They said: The God of the world testified that these actions issued from the servants. And the prophets acknowledged it, and the believers affirmed it, and Iblis the accursed acknowledged it. So whoever denies that has opposed [all?] these. As for the explanation that Allah the Exalted testified to it, it is in verses: (And Samiri led them astray) and this is a declaration that that misguidance is from Samiri. And if the Creator of misguidance were Allah the Exalted, then attributing misguidance to Samiri would be a lie. And it cannot be said: the reading of some of them: (And Samiri led them astray) i.e., the most astray among them is Samiri – because we say: the reading which we hold is correct and authentic by agreement, and it indicates our statement. Thus the objective is achieved. As for the reading which you mentioned, it does not contradict what we have mentioned because Samiri being astray does not negate his being a misleader.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer within a larger discussion of predestination (qadar). The opponents (the 'they' referred to) argue that God, prophets, believers, and even Iblis have testified that actions truly issue from human servants, implying human autonomy. They claim that denying this contradicts universal testimony. The author responds by citing the Qur'anic verse (20:85) 'And Samiri led them astray' to argue that misguidance is attributed to Samiri, not directly to God. If God were the sole creator of all misguidance, attributing it to Samiri would be false. The author then addresses a variant reading ( 'And Samiri led them astray' meaning 'he is the most astray among them') by asserting that their preferred reading is authentic and suffices for the proof. The variant reading does not contradict because being astray does not preclude being a misleader; Samiri is both astray and a misleader. Thus the argument stands that misguidance can be genuinely attributed to the servant, supporting the view that human actions are not simply created by God in a deterministic sense.

Key Terms
أضلهم السامري
إضلال
القراءة
خالق الضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse 'And Samiri led them astray' proves that Samiri created misguidance independently, completely independent of divine will.
Correction: The author uses the verse to show that misguidance is attributable to the servant while still affirming divine sovereignty, not that the servant acts independently.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the implication of the verse 'And Samiri led them astray' if God were the sole creator of misguidance?
A) That God's power is limited.
B) That attributing misguidance to Samiri would be a lie.
C) That Samiri is more powerful than God.
D) That the variant reading is incorrect.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'And Samiri led them astray' demonstrates that misguidance can be genuinely attributed to the servant, supporting the view that human actions are not solely created by God in a deterministic sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into sin, do you honestly own it as your action, or do you subtly think 'Allah wrote it for me' to absolve yourself of responsibility? The Samiri verse shows that misguidance is genuinely from the servant. What would change in your repentance if you fully accepted your role?
?
The author argues that if God were the sole creator of misguidance, then attributing it to Samiri would be a lie. Are there areas in your life where you treat your own bad choices as if God created them without your involvement? How can you begin to practice genuine ownership of your actions?
?
Samiri was both astray and a misleader. Reflect: Are you leading anyone else astray—by your speech, habits, or example—while also being astray yourself? Will you seek guidance so that you are no longer a source of misguidance for others?

Qur'anic Proof for Human Agency
A Qur'anic proof that actions are from servants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars used Qur'anic verses to argue that human actions are truly attributed to humans, not directly to God.

Core Idea
The Qur'an explicitly attributes actions to humans, proving they are real agents.

The author begins by stating that the God of the world, the prophets, believers, and even Iblis all testify that human actions come from the servants. He then provides a detailed list of Qur'anic verses that explicitly assign acts like misguidance, sin, and disbelief to human beings. This lesson explores his key arguments and his response to an objection about a variant recitation of the Samari verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis statement
The author asserts that four witnesses—God, prophets, believers, and Iblis—confirm human agency. Read the opening lines carefully.
This sets up the entire argument: whoever denies this contradicts all four.
2
Examine the Samari verse and objection
Focus on the verse 'And the Samari led them astray.' The author refutes a variant reading that would weaken the argument. Understand how he defends the standard reading.
Note the logic: if God created misguidance, attributing it to Samari would be false.
3
Review the list of supporting verses
The author cites many verses (labeled b through yab) that attribute actions to humans. Read each and see how they prove human agency.
Each verse either explicitly states the act comes 'from themselves' or implies human responsibility.
4
Analyze the refutation at the end
The final verses invalidate the claim that God creates disbelief and then punishes it. The author shows that the Qur'an attributes disbelief to humans.
This closes the argument: if disbelief is from humans, God is not its creator.

The eighteenth type: the value. They said: The God of the world testified that these actions emanated from the servants. And the prophets acknowledged it, and the believers affirmed it, and Iblis the accursed acknowledged it. So whoever denies that has contradicted all of these. As for the exposition that God the Exalted testified to it, it is in verses: A) 'And the Samari led them astray' (2) — and this is an explicit statement that that misguidance was from the Samari, and if the Creator of misguidance were God the Exalted, then attributing misguidance to the Samari would be a lie. And it cannot be said: Some people's recitation: 'And the Samari led them astray' (3), meaning: the most misguided among them is the Samari — because we say: The recitation which we hold is true and correct by consensus, and it indicates what we say. So the purpose has been achieved. As for the recitation you mentioned, it does not contradict what we have said, because the Samari being misguided does not preclude him from being a misguider. (1) omitted (M). (2) Surah Taha, verse 85, and in Tafsir Majma' al-Bayan: 'And the Samari led them astray' meaning he called them to misguidance so they accepted from him, and they went astray at his call. So He attributed misguidance to the Samari and trial to Himself, to indicate that trial is other than misguidance. And it is said: the meaning of 'We tested your people' is We treated them like the one tested and tried to manifest for others the sincere one among them from the hypocrite, so that the sincere one may ally with the sincere and oppose the hypocrite. (3) In Tafsir al-Kashshaf: 'And it was recited: And the Samari led them astray,' meaning he is the most misguided, because he is a misguided misguider, and he is attributed to a tribe of the Children of Israel called the Samara. And it is said: the Samara are a people from the Jews who differ from them in some of their religion.' 345. B) 'You said: Where is this? Say: It is from yourselves' (1) — C) 'And We seized each for his sin' (3) — for if the occurrence of sin were by God's creation, then He would be punishing not for his own sin, but for the sin which another committed in him. D) 'And We did not wrong them, but they were the wrongdoers' (3) — and this is an explicit statement that that is not from God but from them. E) 'God only wants to afflict them with some of their sins' (4) — and how can He afflict them with their sins when they have no sin in those actions which are disbelief and disobedience, just as they have no sin in the existence of heaven and earth? F) 'And Pharaoh misled his people and did not guide them' (9) — and this is an explicit statement that misguidance is from Pharaoh, and if it were from God, it would not be from Pharaoh. Z) 'And Satan wants to lead them far astray' (2) — H) 'Disbelievers out of envy from themselves' (7) — and this is an explicit statement that that action is from themselves. T) God the Exalted related about the people of the Fire calling out to them: 'Were we not with you? They said: Yes, but you tempted yourselves, and waited, and doubted, and your wishes deluded you' (8) — and this is an explicit text that there is no act of God in disbelief. Y) 'Secret counsel is only from Satan to grieve those who believe' (9) — and the word 'only' indicates restriction. Ya) 'Satan made it seem good to them and gave them respite' (10). Yab) 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful and believe?' (11) — and this invalidates the saying of those who say: He created disbelief in the world then punishes for it in the Hereafter, and that He the Exalted only created it for this punishment. (8) (10) And such verses are many, so we suffice with this amount.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) by citation of Qur'anic verses to demonstrate that actions are truly attributed to human beings and other creatures, not directly to God. The author begins by stating the thesis: the God of the world, the prophets, the believers, and Iblis all testify that these actions (i.e., human acts) emanate from the servants. Denying this contradicts all four testimonies. The author then proceeds to the first testimony: that of God. He cites the verse 'And the Samari led them astray' (Taha 20:85). He argues that this verse explicitly attributes misguidance to the Samari. If God were the creator of misguidance, then attributing misguidance to the Samari would be a lie. The author then anticipates an objection: some recite the verse as 'And the Samari led them astray' (with a different reading meaning 'he is the most misguided'), which might imply that the ascription of misguidance is not real but merely a description of the Samari's state. The author refutes this by saying that the accepted reading is authentic and sufficient for the argument. The alternate reading does not contradict because being misguided (ḍāll) does not preclude being a misguider (muḍill). He then provides a long list of other verses (labeled b, c, d, etc.) that attribute actions to human agents, such as 'It is from yourselves' (from Q. 3:165), 'We seized each for his sin' (Q. 29:40), 'We did not wrong them, but they were the wrongdoers' (Q. 43:76), etc. Each verse either explicitly assigns an act to the servant or implies that the servant is the agent, and not God. For example, verse h (Q. 2:109) says 'disbelievers out of envy from themselves,' and the author comments that this is an explicit statement that the act is from themselves. He also cites verses attributing misguidance to Pharaoh and Satan, and a verse from the people of the Fire saying 'you tempted yourselves,' concluding that God has no part in disbelief. The author finally states that these verses invalidate the claim that God creates disbelief and then punishes it, because He only created it for punishment — the verses show that disbelief is from the servants, not from God. The passage is methodical: it states the thesis, then gives a detailed proof from the Qur'an, including a refutation of a potential objection regarding variant readings.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The variant reading 'وأضلهم السامري' (wa-aḍalluhum al-Sāmariyyu) meaning 'the most misguided' completely undermines the argument by denying any real attribution of leading astray to the Samari.
Correction: The author explains that the variant does not contradict his argument because being 'most misguided' (aḍallu) does not prevent him from being a misguider (muḍill); the standard reading remains valid and sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author refute the variant reading of 'وأضلهم السامري' (as 'the most misguided')?
A) He says the variant is unauthentic and should be rejected entirely.
B) He says the variant does not contradict because being misguided does not preclude being a misguider.
C) He says the variant actually strengthens his argument by emphasizing the Samari's sin.
D) He says the variant changes the meaning but both readings support his view equally.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an repeatedly attributes actions to human beings, proving that they are genuine agents responsible for their deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'It was meant to be' or 'God decreed it'? After reading these verses—especially 'It is from yourselves'—what will you tell yourself the next time you are tempted to blame fate? Will you take full ownership of your choice?
?
The verse 'But you tempted yourselves' (t) confronts you directly. In what area of your life are you currently 'tempting yourself' while pretending it is beyond your control? What would it cost you to admit it is your own doing, and to stop?
?
If you truly believe that your actions are yours and not forced by God, how should that change the way you pray for forgiveness? Are you ready to stand before Him and say 'I did this' without shifting blame, trusting that He is just and merciful? When will you practice that honesty in your next du'a?

Proof from Quran: Sin Comes from the Self
Author presents four Quranic proofs that sin is from the self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how four Quranic verses directly attribute sin and misguidance to human beings themselves, refuting the idea that Allah creates sins.

Core Idea
The Quran repeatedly says sin is from us, not from Allah.

The author begins by citing a series of Quranic verses that clearly place responsibility for sin on human beings. Each verse is accompanied by a brief commentary showing how it disproves the claim that Allah creates disbelief and then punishes for it. This lesson covers the first four proofs, establishing that sin originates from human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses and commentary
Read the Arabic passage with its translation. Notice how each verse is followed by an immediate logical inference.
The verses are labeled ب, ج, د, هـ.
2
Identify the shared theme
All four verses emphasize that sin or punishment is 'from yourselves' or 'from them'. See how the author uses each to argue against divine creation of sin.
3
Understand the reasoning for verse c
The author argues that if sin were created by Allah, He would be punishing for another's act. Understand this reasoning.
This is a key logical move.
4
Reflect on the question in verse e
The author asks how Allah can afflict them with their sins if they have no sin in those acts. Think about the implication.
It challenges the idea that disbelief is created.

B. You said: 'Whence is this?' Say: 'It is from yourselves' (1). C. 'And each We seized for his sin' (2). If sin came about by Allah’s creation, He would be taking one to task not for his own sin, but for the sin that another did in him. D. 'And We did not wrong them, but they were the wrongdoers' (3). This is an explicit statement that that is not from Allah, but from them. E. 'Allah only desires to afflict them with some of their sins' (4). How can He afflict them with their sins when they have no sin in those acts which are disbelief and disobedience, just as they have no sin in the existence of the heavens and the earth? F. 'And Pharaoh led his people astray and did not guide [them]' (5). This is an explicit statement that misguidance is from Pharaoh; had it been from Allah, it would not be from Pharaoh. G. 'And Satan desires to lead them far astray' (6). H. 'Disbelievers, out of envy from themselves' (7). This is an explicit statement that that act is from themselves. T. Allah Almighty said, narrating about the people of the Fire calling out to them: 'Were we not with you?' They said: 'Yes, but you tempted yourselves, and waited, and doubted, and delusions deceived you' (8). This is an explicit text that Allah has no hand in disbelief. Y. 'Private conversation is only from Satan, to grieve those who believe' (9). The word 'innama' indicates restriction. K. 'Satan tempted them and gave them false hope' (10). L. 'What would Allah do with your punishment if you are grateful and believe?' (11). This invalidates the saying of whoever says: He created disbelief in the world, then punishes for it in the Hereafter, and that the Exalted only created it for this punishment. And similar verses are many; we suffice with this amount.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a series of proofs from the Quran refuting the Jabriyya claim that sins are created by Allah and then He punishes people for them. The author proceeds alphabetically, citing each verse and immediately explaining how it disproves the Jabriyya position. The first verse (Al-Imran 3:165) commands the Prophet to say that what befell them is from themselves. The second (Al-Ankabut 29:40) states that each was seized for his own sin, implying responsibility. The author argues: if sin were created by Allah, He would be punishing for another's act. Next (Az-Zukhruf 43:76) explicitly says they wronged themselves, not Allah. Then (Al-Ma'idah 5:50) says Allah only afflicts them with some of their sins, raising the question: how can He afflict them for sins they did not commit (if He created their disbelief)? Next (Ta-Ha 20:79) says Pharaoh led his people astray, so misguidance is attributed to Pharaoh. Then (An-Nisa 4:60) mentions Satan's desire to lead astray. Then (Al-Baqarah 2:109) says the disbelief was from envy from themselves. Then (Al-Hadid 57:14) has the people of the Fire admitting they tempted themselves. Then (Al-Mujadilah 58:10) uses 'innama' to restrict evil conversation to Satan. Then (Muhammad 47:25) says Satan tempted them. Finally (An-Nisa 4:147) asks what Allah would gain from punishing them if they are grateful and believe, demonstrating that punishment is contingent on their own actions, not on a predetermined creation of disbelief. The author concludes that many such verses exist and he has sufficed with this amount.

الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah creates disbelief in a person and then punishes them for it.
Correction: The Quran verses show that sin is attributed to the person themselves, not to Allah's creation.

C) Angels wronged them
D) Satan wronged them

Key Takeaway
The Quran consistently places responsibility for sin on human beings, not on Allah's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you genuinely accept that it is from yourself, or do you find yourself blaming circumstances or even Allah's decree? How would your repentance change if you fully owned your sin?
?
The verse says 'We did not wrong them, but they were the wrongdoers.' In what areas of your life are you tempted to feel wronged by Allah when in reality you are the wrongdoer? Will you repent today?
?
If you truly believed that every sin is from your own self, what would you stop doing? What would you start doing differently?
